In each locality, they governed themselves, spoke their own language, ran their own schools, cultural and religious institutions, and paid somewhat higher taxes. They had no power outside the millet. The Imperial government protected them and prevented major violent clashes between ethnic groups.\\n

Ethnic nationalism, based on distinctive religion and language, provided a centripetal force that eventually destroyed the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert1983_275-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert1983-275\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>260<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> In addition, Muslim ethnic groups, which were not part of the millet system, especially the Arabs and the Kurds, were outside the Turkish culture and developed their own separate nationalism. The British sponsored Arab nationalism in the First World War, promising an independent Arab state in return for Arab support. Most Arabs supported the Sultan, but those near Mecca believed in and supported the British promise.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-276\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-276\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>261<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Istanbul_asv2021-11_img03_Hemdat_Israel_Synagogue.jpg/250px-Istanbul_asv2021-11_img03_Hemdat_Israel_Synagogue.jpg/%22)

[Hemdat Israel Synagogue](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Hemdat_Israel_Synagogue/%22 "\\"Hemdat") of [Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Istanbul/%22 "\\"Istanbul\\"")

\\n

At the local level, power was held beyond the control of the Sultan by the [ayans](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ayan_\(class\)/%22 "\\"Ayan") or local notables. The ayan collected taxes, formed local armies to compete with other notables, took a reactionary attitude toward political or economic change, and often defied policies handed down by the Sultan.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-277\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-277\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>262<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

After the 18th century, the Ottoman Empire was shrinking, as Russia put on heavy pressure and expanded to its south; Egypt became effectively independent in 1805, and the British later took it over, along with Cyprus. Greece became independent, and Serbia and other Balkan areas became highly restive as the force of nationalism pushed against imperialism. The French took over Algeria and Tunisia. The Europeans all thought that the empire was a sick man in rapid decline. Only the Germans seemed helpful, and their support led to the Ottoman Empire joining the central powers in 1915, with the result that they came out as one of the heaviest losers of the First World War in 1918.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-278\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-278\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>263<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

## Culture

\\n

Main article: [Culture of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Culture_of_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Culture")

\\n\\n\\n\\n\\n\\n

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/250px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif/%22)

Depiction of a [hookah](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Hookah/%22 "\\"Hookah\\"") shop in [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Lebanon/%22 "\\"Lebanon\\"")

\\n

The Ottomans absorbed some of the traditions, art, and institutions of cultures in the regions they conquered and added new dimensions to them. Numerous traditions and cultural traits of previous empires (in fields such as architecture, cuisine, music, leisure, and government) were adopted by the Ottoman Turks, who developed them into new forms, resulting in a new and distinctively Ottoman cultural identity. Although the predominant literary language of the Ottoman Empire was Turkish, Persian was the preferred vehicle for the projection of an imperial image.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-279\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-279\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>264<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

[Slavery](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Slavery") was part of Ottoman society,<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-280\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-280\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>265<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> with most slaves employed as domestic servants. Agricultural slavery, like that in the Americas, was relatively rare. Unlike systems of [chattel slavery](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Slavery/%22 "\\"Slavery\\""), slaves under Islamic law were not regarded as movable property, and the children of female slaves were born legally free. Female slaves were still sold in the Empire as late as 1908.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-281\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-281\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>266<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> During the 19th century the Empire came under pressure from Western European countries to outlaw the practice. Policies developed by various sultans throughout the 19th century attempted to curtail the [Ottoman slave trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_slave_trade/%22 "\\"Ottoman") but slavery had centuries of religious backing and sanction and so was never abolished in the Empire.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Syed-2011_264-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Syed-2011-264\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>249<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

Ottomans adopted Persian bureaucratic traditions and culture. The sultans also made an important contribution in the development of Persian literature.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-282\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-282\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>267<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

Language was not an obvious sign of group connection and identity in the 16th century among the rulers of the Ottoman Empire, [Safavid Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Safavid_Iran/%22 "\\"Safavid") and [Abu'l-Khayrid Shibanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Shaybanids/%22 "\\"Shaybanids\\"") of [Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Central_Asia/%22 "\\"Central").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Comstock-Skipp-2023_283-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Comstock-Skipp-2023-283\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>268<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Hence the ruling classes of all three polities were bilingual in varieties of Persian and Turkic.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Comstock-Skipp-2023_283-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Comstock-Skipp-2023-283\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>268<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> But in the century's final quarter, linguistic adjustments occurred in the Ottoman and Safavid realms defined by a new rigidity that favoured Ottoman Turkish and Persian, respectively.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Comstock-Skipp-2023_283-2\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Comstock-Skipp-2023-283\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>268<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Education

\\n

Main article: [Education in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Education_in_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Education")

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg/250px-Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg/%22)

The Beyazıt State Library was founded in 1884.

\\n

In the Ottoman Empire, each [millet](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Millet_\(Ottoman_Empire\)/%22 "\\"Millet") established a schooling system serving its members.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Strauss-2016_284-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Strauss-2016-284\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>269<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Education was therefore largely divided on ethnic and religious lines: few non-Muslims attended schools for Muslim students, and vice versa. Most institutions that served all ethnic and religious groups taught in French or other languages.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-StraussPT195_285-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-StraussPT195-285\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>270<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

Several \\"foreign schools\\" (*Frerler mektebleri*) operated by religious clergy primarily served Christians, although some Muslim students attended.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Strauss-2016_284-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Strauss-2016-284\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>269<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Garnett described the schools for Christians and Jews as \\"organised upon European models\\", with \\"voluntary contributions\\" supporting their operation and most of them \\"well attended\\" and with \\"a high standard of education\\".<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-286\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-286\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>271<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Literature

\\n

Main article: [Ottoman literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_literature/%22 "\\"Ottoman")

\\n

The two primary streams of Ottoman written literature are poetry and [prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Prose/%22 "\\"Prose\\""). Poetry was by far the dominant stream. The earliest work of Ottoman historiography for example, the [*İskendernâme*](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Iskendername/%22 "\\"Iskendername\\""), was composed by the poet [Taceddin Ahmedi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Taceddin_Ahmedi/%22 "\\"Taceddin").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-:1_287-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22:1-287\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>272<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Until the 19th century, Ottoman prose did not contain any examples of fiction: there were no counterparts to, for instance, the European [romance](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Chivalric_romance/%22 "\\"Chivalric"), short story, or novel. Analog genres did exist, though, in both [Turkish folk literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkish_folk_literature/%22 "\\"Turkish") and in [Divan poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Diwan_\(poetry\)/%22 "\\"Diwan").\\n

Ottoman Divan poetry was a highly ritualized and symbolic art form. From the [Persian poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Persian_literature/%22 "\\"Persian") that largely inspired it, it inherited a wealth of symbols whose meanings and interrelationships—both of similitude (مراعات نظير *mura'ât-i nazîr* / تناسب *tenâsüb*) and opposition (تضاد *tezâd*) were more or less prescribed. Divan poetry was composed through the constant juxtaposition of many such images within a strict metrical framework, allowing numerous potential meanings to emerge. The vast majority of Divan poetry was [lyric](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Lyric_poetry/%22 "\\"Lyric") in nature: either [gazels](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Gazel/%22 "\\"Gazel\\"") (which make up the greatest part of the repertoire of the tradition), or kasîdes. But there were other common genres, especially the mesnevî, a kind of [verse romance](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Courtly_romance/%22 "\\"Courtly") and thus a variety of [narrative poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Narrative_poetry/%22 "\\"Narrative"); the two most notable examples of this form are the [Leyli and Majnun](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Leyli_and_Majnun_\(Fuzuli\)/%22 "\\"Leyli") of [Fuzuli](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Fuzuli_\(poet\)/%22 "\\"Fuzuli") and the [Hüsn ü Aşk](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/H%C3%BCsn_%C3%BC_A%C5%9Fk/%22 "\\"Hüsn") of [Şeyh Gâlib](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/%C5%9Eeyh_G%C3%A2lib/%22 "\\"Şeyh"). The [Seyahatnâme](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Seyahatn%C3%A2me/%22 "\\"Seyahatnâme\\"") of [Evliya Çelebi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi/%22 "\\"Evliya") (1611–1682) is an outstanding example of travel literature.\\n

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/250px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/%22)

[Ahmet Nedîm Efendi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ned%C3%AEm/%22 "\\"Nedîm\\""), one of the most celebrated Ottoman poets

\\n

Until the 19th century, [Ottoman prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Prose_of_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Prose") did not develop to the extent that contemporary Divan poetry did. A large part of the reason was that much prose was expected to adhere to the rules of *sec* (سجع, also transliterated as *seci*), or [rhymed prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Rhymed_prose/%22 "\\"Rhymed"),<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-288\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-288\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>273<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> a type of writing descended from the Arabic [saj'](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Saj%27/%22 "\\"Saj'\\"") that prescribed that between each adjective and noun in a string of words, such as a sentence, there must be a rhyme. Nevertheless, there was a tradition of prose in the literature of the time, though it was exclusively nonfictional. One apparent exception was [Muhayyelât](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Giritli_Ali_Aziz_Efendi/%22 "\\"Giritli") (*Fancies*) by [Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Giritli_Ali_Aziz_Efendi/%22 "\\"Giritli"), a collection of stories of the fantastic written in 1796, though not published until 1867. The first novel published in the Ottoman Empire was [Vartan Pasha](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Vartan_Pasha/%22 "\\"Vartan")'s 1851 *The Story of Akabi* (Turkish: *Akabi Hikyayesi*). It was written in Turkish but with [Armenian](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Armenian_language/%22 "\\"Armenian") script.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-289\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-289\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>274<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-290\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-290\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>275<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-291\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-291\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>276<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-292\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-292\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>277<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

Due to historically close ties with France, [French literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/French_literature/%22 "\\"French") constituted the major Western influence on Ottoman literature in the latter half of the 19th century. As a result, many of the same movements prevalent in France during this period had Ottoman equivalents; in the developing Ottoman prose tradition, for instance, the influence of [Romanticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Romanticism/%22 "\\"Romanticism\\"") can be seen during the Tanzimat period, and that of the [Realist](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Realism_\(arts\)/%22 "\\"Realism") and [Naturalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Naturalism_\(literature\)/%22 "\\"Naturalism") movements in subsequent periods; in the poetic tradition, on the other hand, the influence of the [Symbolist](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Symbolism_\(arts\)/%22 "\\"Symbolism") and [Parnassian](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Parnassianism/%22 "\\"Parnassianism\\"") movements was paramount.\\n

Many of the writers in the Tanzimat period wrote in several different genres simultaneously. This diversity was, in part, due to Tanzimat writers' wish to disseminate as much of the new literature as possible, in the hopes that it would contribute to a revitalization of Ottoman [social structures](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Social_structure/%22 "\\"Social").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-293\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-293\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>278<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Media

\\n

Main article: [Media of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Media_of_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Media")

\\n

The media of the Ottoman Empire was diverse, with newspapers and journals published in languages including [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/French_\(language\)/%22 "\\"French"),<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-BaruhMusnik_294-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-BaruhMusnik-294\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>279<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Greek_\(language\)/%22 "\\"Greek"),<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-StraussConstp32_295-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-StraussConstp32-295\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>280<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> and [German](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/German_\(language\)/%22 "\\"German").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Syed-2011_264-2\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Syed-2011-264\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>249<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Many of these publications were centered in [Constantinople](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Constantinople/%22 "\\"Constantinople\\""),<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Kendallp339_296-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Kendallp339-296\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>281<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> but there were also French-language newspapers produced in [Beirut](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Beirut/%22 "\\"Beirut\\""), [Salonika](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Salonika/%22 "\\"Salonika\\""), and [Smyrna](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Smyrna/%22 "\\"Smyrna\\"").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-StraussLanguagep122_297-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-StraussLanguagep122-297\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>282<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Non-Muslim ethnic minorities in the empire used French as a [lingua franca](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Lingua_franca/%22 "\\"Lingua") and used French-language publications,<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-BaruhMusnik_294-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-BaruhMusnik-294\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>279<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> while some provincial newspapers were published in [Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Arabic/%22 "\\"Arabic\\"").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-StraussConstp25_298-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-StraussConstp25-298\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>283<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The use of French in the media persisted until the [end of the empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Dissolution") in 1923 and for a few years thereafter in the [Republic of Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkey/%22 "\\"Turkey\\"").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-BaruhMusnik_294-2\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-BaruhMusnik-294\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>279<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Architecture

\\n

Main article: [Ottoman architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_architecture/%22 "\\"Ottoman")

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/250px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/%22)

[Süleymaniye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque/%22 "\\"Süleymaniye") in Istanbul, designed by [Mimar Sinan](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mimar_Sinan/%22 "\\"Mimar") in the 16th century and a major example of the [classical Ottoman style](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Classical_Ottoman_architecture/%22 "\\"Classical")

\\n

The architecture of the empire developed from earlier [Seljuk Turkish architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Anatolian_Seljuk_architecture/%22 "\\"Anatolian"), with influences from [Byzantine](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Byzantine_architecture/%22 "\\"Byzantine") and [Iranian](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Iranian_architecture/%22 "\\"Iranian") architecture and other architectural traditions in the Middle East.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-299\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-299\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>284<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Bloom-2009_300-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Bloom-2009-300\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>285<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Freely-2011_301-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Freely-2011-301\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>286<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> [Early Ottoman architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Early_Ottoman_architecture/%22 "\\"Early") experimented with multiple building types over the course of the 13th to 15th centuries, progressively evolving into the [classical Ottoman style](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Classical_Ottoman_architecture/%22 "\\"Classical") of the 16th and 17th centuries, which was also strongly influenced by the [Hagia Sophia](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Hagia_Sophia/%22 "\\"Hagia").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Freely-2011_301-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Freely-2011-301\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>286<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Goodwin_302-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Goodwin-302\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>287<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The most important architect of the Classical period is [Mimar Sinan](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mimar_Sinan/%22 "\\"Mimar"), whose major works include the [Şehzade Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/%C5%9Eehzade_Mosque/%22 "\\"Şehzade"), [Süleymaniye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque/%22 "\\"Süleymaniye"), and [Selimiye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Selimiye_Mosque,_Edirne/%22 "\\"Selimiye").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-AgostonMasters20102_303-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-AgostonMasters20102-303\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>288<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Grove_encycl-Ottoman_304-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Grove_encycl-Ottoman-304\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>289<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The second half of the 16th century also saw the apogee of some [types of decoration](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_decoration/%22 "\\"Ottoman"), most notably in the production of [Iznik tiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Iznik_pottery/%22 "\\"Iznik").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-305\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-305\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>290<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mihrisah_Sultan_Complex_Eyup_DSCF7829.jpg/250px-Mihrisah_Sultan_Complex_Eyup_DSCF7829.jpg/%22)

[Sebil](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Sebil/%22 "\\"Sebil\\"") (water distribution kiosk) of the [Mihrişah Sultan Complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mihri%C5%9Fah_Sultan_Complex/%22 "\\"Mihrişah") in Istanbul, from the late 18th century, an example of the [Ottoman Baroque style](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_Baroque_style/%22 "\\"Ottoman")

\\n

Beginning in the 18th century, Ottoman architecture was opened to external influences, particularly from [Baroque architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Baroque_architecture/%22 "\\"Baroque") in Western Europe, which eventually gave rise to the [Ottoman Baroque](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_Baroque_architecture/%22 "\\"Ottoman") style that emerged in the 1740s.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTERüstem201921–70_(see_chapters_1_and_2_generally)_306-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%BCstem201921%E2%80%9370_(see_chapters_1_and_2_generally)-306\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>291<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The [Nuruosmaniye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Nuruosmaniye_Mosque/%22 "\\"Nuruosmaniye") is one of the most important examples from this period.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011355_307-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011355-307\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>292<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010526_308-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010526-308\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>293<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> During the 19th century, influences from Western Europe became more prominent, brought in by architects such as those from the [Balyan family](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Balyan_family/%22 "\\"Balyan").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393_309-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393-309\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>294<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> [Empire style](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Empire_style/%22 "\\"Empire") and [Neoclassical](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture/%22 "\\"Neoclassical") motifs were introduced and a trend towards [eclecticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Eclecticism_in_architecture/%22 "\\"Eclecticism") was evident in many types of buildings, such as the [Dolmabaçe Palace](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace/%22 "\\"Dolmabahçe").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010605–606_310-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010605%E2%80%93606-310\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>295<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The last decades of the Ottoman Empire also saw the development of a new architectural style called neo-Ottoman or Ottoman [revivalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Revivalism_\(architecture\)/%22 "\\"Revivalism"), also known as the [First National Architectural Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/First_national_architectural_movement/%22 "\\"First"),<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Bloom-2009a_311-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Bloom-2009a-311\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>296<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> by architects such as [Mimar Kemaleddin](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mimar_Kemaleddin/%22 "\\"Mimar") and [Vedat Tek](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Vedat_Tek/%22 "\\"Vedat").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393_309-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393-309\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>294<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

Ottoman dynastic patronage was concentrated in the historic capitals of Bursa, Edirne, and Istanbul (Constantinople), as well as in several other important administrative centers such as [Amasya](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Amasya/%22 "\\"Amasya\\"") and [Manisa](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Manisa/%22 "\\"Manisa\\""). It was in these centers that most important developments in Ottoman architecture occurred and that the most monumental Ottoman architecture can be found.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010679_312-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010679-312\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>297<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Major religious monuments were typically architectural complexes, known as a *[külliye](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/K%C3%BClliye/%22 "\\"Külliye\\"")*, that had multiple components providing different services or amenities. In addition to a mosque, these could include a [madrasa](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Madrasa/%22 "\\"Madrasa\\""), a [hammam](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Hammam/%22 "\\"Hammam\\""), an [imaret](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Imaret/%22 "\\"Imaret\\""), a [sebil](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Sebil_\(fountain\)/%22 "\\"Sebil"), a market, a [caravanserai](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Caravanserai/%22 "\\"Caravanserai\\""), a [primary school](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Kuttab/%22 "\\"Kuttab\\""), or others.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010_313-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010-313\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>298<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> These complexes were governed and managed with the help of a *[vakıf](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Vak%C4%B1f/%22 "\\"Vakıf\\"")* agreement (Arabic *waqf*).<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010_313-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010-313\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>298<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Ottoman constructions were still abundant in Anatolia and in the Balkans (Rumelia), but in the more distant Middle Eastern and North African provinces older [Islamic architectural](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Islamic_architecture/%22 "\\"Islamic") styles continued to hold strong influence and were sometimes blended with Ottoman styles.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010571–596_314-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010571%E2%80%93596-314\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>299<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlairBloom1995251_315-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlairBloom1995251-315\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>300<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Decorative arts

\\n

Further information: [Ottoman illumination](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_illumination/%22 "\\"Ottoman") and [Ottoman miniature](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_miniature/%22 "\\"Ottoman")

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg/250px-Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg/%22)

[Ottoman miniature](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_miniature/%22 "\\"Ottoman") lost its function with the Westernization of Ottoman culture.

\\n

The tradition of [Ottoman miniatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_miniature/%22 "\\"Ottoman"), painted to illustrate manuscripts or used in dedicated albums, was heavily influenced by the [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Persian_miniature/%22 "\\"Persian") art form, though it also included elements of the [Byzantine](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Byzantine_art/%22 "\\"Byzantine") tradition of [illumination](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript/%22 "\\"Illuminated") and painting.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-316\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-316\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>301<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> A Greek academy of painters, the *Nakkashane-i-Rum*, was established in the [Topkapi Palace](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace/%22 "\\"Topkapı") in the 15th century, while early in the following century a similar Persian academy, the *Nakkashane-i-Irani*, was added. [Surname-i Hümayun](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Surname-i_H%C3%BCmayun/%22 "\\"Surname-i") (Imperial Festival Books) were albums that commemorated celebrations in the Ottoman Empire in pictorial and textual detail.\\n

[Ottoman illumination](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_illumination/%22 "\\"Ottoman") covers non-figurative painted or drawn decorative art in books or on sheets in *[muraqqa](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Muraqqa/%22 "\\"Muraqqa\\"")* or albums, as opposed to the figurative images of the [Ottoman miniature](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_miniature/%22 "\\"Ottoman"). It was a part of the Ottoman Book Arts together with the Ottoman miniature (*taswir*), calligraphy (*hat*), [Islamic calligraphy](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy/%22 "\\"Islamic"), bookbinding (*cilt*) and [paper marbling](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Paper_marbling/%22 "\\"Paper") (*ebru*). In the Ottoman Empire, [illuminated and illustrated manuscripts](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript/%22 "\\"Illuminated") were commissioned by the Sultan or the administrators of the court. In Topkapi Palace, these manuscripts were created by the artists working in *Nakkashane*, the atelier of the miniature and illumination artists. Both religious and non-religious books could be illuminated. Also, sheets for albums *levha* consisted of illuminated calligraphy (*hat*) of *[tughra](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Tughra/%22 "\\"Tughra\\"")*, religious texts, verses from poems or proverbs, and purely decorative drawings.\\n

The art of carpet [weaving](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Weaving/%22 "\\"Weaving\\"") was particularly significant in the Ottoman Empire, carpets having an immense importance both as decorative furnishings, rich in religious and other symbolism and as a practical consideration, as it was customary to remove one's shoes in living quarters.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-foroqhi_317-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-foroqhi-317\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>302<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The weaving of such carpets originated in the [nomadic](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Nomad/%22 "\\"Nomad\\"") cultures of central Asia (carpets being an easily transportable form of furnishing), and eventually spread to the settled societies of Anatolia. Turks used carpets, rugs, and [kilims](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Kilim/%22 "\\"Kilim\\"") not just on the floors of a room but also as a hanging on walls and doorways, where they provided additional insulation. They were also commonly donated to mosques, which often amassed large collections of them.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-foroqhip153_318-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-foroqhip153-318\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>303<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Music and performing arts

\\n

Further information: [Ottoman Music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_Music/%22 "\\"Ottoman")

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Musicians_and_dancers_from_ottoman_empire.jpg/250px-Musicians_and_dancers_from_ottoman_empire.jpg/%22)

Musicians and dancers entertaining the crowds, from the [Surname-i Hümayun](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Surname-i_H%C3%BCmayun/%22 "\\"Surname-i"), 1720

\\n

[Ottoman classical music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_music/%22 "\\"Ottoman") was an important part of the education of the Ottoman elite. A number of the Ottoman sultans have accomplished musicians and composers themselves, such as [Selim III](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Selim_III/%22 "\\"Selim"), whose compositions are often still performed today. Ottoman classical music arose largely from a confluence of [Byzantine music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Byzantine_music/%22 "\\"Byzantine"), [Armenian music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Music_of_Armenia/%22 "\\"Music"), [Arabic music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Arabic_music/%22 "\\"Arabic"), and [Persian music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Persian_traditional_music/%22 "\\"Persian"). Compositionally, it is organized around rhythmic units called [usul](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Usul_\(music\)/%22 "\\"Usul"), which are somewhat similar to [meter](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Metre_\(music\)/%22 "\\"Metre") in Western music, and [melodic](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Melody/%22 "\\"Melody\\"") units called [makam](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkish_makam/%22 "\\"Turkish"), which bear some resemblance to Western [musical modes](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mode_\(music\)/%22 "\\"Mode").\\n

The [instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Musical_instrument/%22 "\\"Musical") used are a mixture of Anatolian and Central Asian instruments (the [saz](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ba%C4%9Flama/%22 "\\"Bağlama\\""), the [bağlama](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ba%C4%9Flama/%22 "\\"Bağlama\\""), the [kemence](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Kemenche/%22 "\\"Kemenche\\"")), other Middle Eastern instruments (the [ud](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Oud/%22 "\\"Oud\\""), the [tanbur](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Tanbur/%22 "\\"Tanbur\\""), the [kanun](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Qanun_\(instrument\)/%22 "\\"Qanun"), the [ney](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ney/%22 "\\"Ney\\"")), and—later in the tradition—Western instruments (the violin and the piano). Because of a geographic and cultural divide between the capital and other areas, two broadly distinct styles of music arose in the Ottoman Empire: Ottoman classical music and folk music. In the provinces, several different kinds of [folk music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Folk_music/%22 "\\"Folk") were created. The most dominant regions with their distinguished musical styles are Balkan-Thracian Türküs, North-Eastern ([Laz](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Laz_people/%22 "\\"Laz")) Türküs, Aegean Türküs, Central Anatolian Türküs, Eastern Anatolian Türküs, and Caucasian Türküs. Some of the distinctive styles were: [Janissary music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_military_band/%22 "\\"Ottoman"), [Roma music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Romani_music/%22 "\\"Romani"), [Belly dance](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Belly_dance/%22 "\\"Belly"), [Turkish folk music](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkish_folk_music/%22 "\\"Turkish").\\n

The traditional [shadow play](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Shadow_play/%22 "\\"Shadow") called [Karagöz and Hacivat](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Karag%C3%B6z_and_Hacivat/%22 "\\"Karagöz") was widespread throughout the Ottoman Empire and featured characters representing all of the major ethnic and social groups in that culture.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-319\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-319\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>304<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-320\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-320\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>305<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> It was performed by a single puppet master, who voiced all of the characters, and accompanied by [tambourine](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Tambourine/%22 "\\"Tambourine\\"") (*def*). Its origins are obscure, deriving perhaps from an older Egyptian tradition, or possibly from an Asian source.\\n

\\n

### Cuisine

\\n

Main article: [Ottoman cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine/%22 "\\"Ottoman")

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/250px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/%22)

Turkish women baking bread, 1790

\\n

[Ottoman cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine/%22 "\\"Ottoman") is the cuisine of the capital, Constantinople ([Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Istanbul/%22 "\\"Istanbul\\"")), and the regional capital cities, where the melting pot of cultures created a common cuisine that most of the population regardless of ethnicity shared. This diverse cuisine was honed in the Imperial Palace's kitchens by chefs brought from certain parts of the Empire to create and experiment with different ingredients. The creations of the Ottoman Palace's kitchens filtered to the population, for instance through [Ramadan](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ramadan/%22 "\\"Ramadan\\"") events, and through the cooking at the [Yalıs](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Yal%C4%B1/%22 "\\"Yalı\\"") of the [Pashas](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Pasha/%22 "\\"Pasha\\""), and from there on spread to the rest of the population.\\n

Much of the cuisine of former Ottoman territories today is descended from a shared Ottoman cuisine, especially [Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkish_cuisine/%22 "\\"Turkish"), and including [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Greek_cuisine/%22 "\\"Greek"), [Balkan](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Balkan_cuisine/%22 "\\"Balkan"), [Armenian](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Armenian_cuisine/%22 "\\"Armenian"), and [Middle Eastern](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Middle_Eastern_cuisine/%22 "\\"Middle") cuisines.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Fragner_321-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Fragner-321\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>306<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n

### Sports

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/MedyaBJK.jpg/250px-MedyaBJK.jpg/%22)

Members of [Beşiktaş J.K.](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K./%22 "\\"Beşiktaş") in 1903

\\n

The main sports Ottomans were engaged in were [Turkish wrestling](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Wrestling_in_Turkey/%22 "\\"Wrestling"), hunting, [Turkish archery](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkish_archery/%22 "\\"Turkish"), horseback riding, [equestrian javelin throw](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Jereed/%22 "\\"Jereed\\""), arm wrestling, and swimming. European model sports clubs were formed with the spreading popularity of [football](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Association_football/%22 "\\"Association") matches in 19th century Constantinople. The leading clubs, according to timeline, were [Beşiktaş Gymnastics Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K./%22 "\\"Beşiktaş") (1903), [Galatasaray Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Galatasaray_S.K./%22 "\\"Galatasaray") (1905), [Fenerbahçe Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Fenerbah%C3%A7e_S.K./%22 "\\"Fenerbahçe") (1907), [MKE Ankaragücü (formerly Turan Sanatkarangücü)](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/MKE_Ankarag%C3%BCc%C3%BC/%22 "\\"MKE") (1910) in Constantinople. Football clubs were formed in other provinces too, such as [Karşıyaka Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Kar%C5%9F%C4%B1yaka_S.K./%22 "\\"Karşıyaka") (1912), [Altay Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Altay_S.K./%22 "\\"Altay") (1914) and [Turkish Fatherland Football Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor/%22 "\\"Ülküspor\\"") (later [Ülküspor](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor/%22 "\\"Ülküspor\\"")) (1914) of [İzmir](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/%C4%B0zmir/%22 "\\"İzmir\\"").\\n

\\n

## Science and technology

\\n

Main article: [Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_the_Ottoman_Empire/%22 "\\"Science")

![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/250px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/%22)

The [Constantinople observatory of Taqi ad-Din](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Constantinople_observatory_of_Taqi_ad-Din/%22 "\\"Constantinople") in 1577

\\n

Over the course of Ottoman history, the Ottomans managed to build a large collection of libraries complete with translations of books from other cultures, as well as original manuscripts.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Ágoston_and_Alan_Masters583_73-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22%C3%81goston_and_Alan_Masters583-73\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>59<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> A great part of this desire for local and foreign manuscripts arose in the 15th century. [Sultan Mehmet II](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mehmed_II/%22 "\\"Mehmed") ordered [Georgios Amiroutzes](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/George_Amiroutzes/%22 "\\"George"), a Greek scholar from [Trabzon](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Trabzon/%22 "\\"Trabzon\\""), to translate and make available to Ottoman educational institutions the geography book of [Ptolemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ptolemy/%22 "\\"Ptolemy\\""). Another example is [Ali Qushji](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ali_Qushji/%22 "\\"Ali") – an [astronomer](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world/%22 "\\"Astronomy"), [mathematician](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mathematics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world/%22 "\\"Mathematics") and [physicist](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Physics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world/%22 "\\"Physics") originally from [Samarkand](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Samarkand/%22 "\\"Samarkand\\"") – who became a professor in two madrasas and influenced Ottoman circles as a result of his writings and the activities of his students, even though he only spent two or three years in Constantinople before his death.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-322\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-322\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>307<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

[Taqi al-Din](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Taqi_ad-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf/%22 "\\"Taqi") built the [Constantinople observatory of Taqi ad-Din](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Constantinople_observatory_of_Taqi_ad-Din/%22 "\\"Constantinople") in 1577, where he carried out observations until 1580. He calculated the [eccentricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity/%22 "\\"Orbital") of the Sun's orbit and the annual motion of the [apogee](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Apsis/%22 "\\"Apsis\\"").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Tekeli_323-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Tekeli-323\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>308<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> However, the observatory's primary purpose was almost certainly [astrological](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Astrology/%22 "\\"Astrology\\"") rather than astronomical, leading to its destruction in 1580 due to the rise of a clerical faction that opposed its use for that purpose.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-324\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-324\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>309<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> He also experimented with [steam power](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Steam_engine/%22 "\\"Steam") in [Ottoman Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet/%22 "\\"Egypt") in 1551, when he described a [steam jack](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Roasting_jack/%22 "\\"Roasting") driven by a rudimentary [steam turbine](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Steam_turbine/%22 "\\"Steam").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-325\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-325\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>310<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n![](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Young_woman_reading_%281880%29%2C_by_Osman_Hamdi_Bey.jpg/250px-Young_woman_reading_%281880%29%2C_by_Osman_Hamdi_Bey.jpg/%22)

*Girl Reciting the Qurān* (*Kuran Okuyan Kız*), an 1880 painting by the Ottoman polymath [Osman Hamdi Bey](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Osman_Hamdi_Bey/%22 "\\"Osman"), whose works often showed women engaged in educational activities<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-326\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-326\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>311<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>

\\n

[Şerafeddin Sabuncuoğlu](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Sabuncuo%C4%9Flu_%C5%9Eerafeddin/%22 "\\"Sabuncuoğlu") was the author of the first surgical atlas and the last major [medical encyclopaedia from the Islamic world](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world/%22 "\\"Medicine").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-327\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-327\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>312<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> The Ottoman Empire is credited with the invention of several surgical instruments in use such as [forceps](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Forceps/%22 "\\"Forceps\\""), [catheters](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Catheter/%22 "\\"Catheter\\""), [scalpels and lancets](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Scalpel/%22 "\\"Scalpel\\"") as well as [pincers](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Pincers_\(tool\)/%22 "\\"Pincers").<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-328\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-328\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>313<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup><sup class="\&quot;noprint" inline-template="" noprint="" noexcerpt="" template-fact\"="" style="\&quot;white-space:nowrap;\&quot;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS/%22" class="\&quot;mw-redirect\&quot;" title="\&quot;Wikipedia:NOTRS\&quot;"><span title="\&quot;This" claim="" needs="" references="" to="" better="" sources.="" (april="" 2022)\"="">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\\n

In the early 19th century, [Egypt under Muhammad Ali](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/History_of_Egypt_under_the_Muhammad_Ali_dynasty/%22 "\\"History") began using [steam engines](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Steam_engine/%22 "\\"Steam") for industrial manufacturing, with industries such as [ironworks](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ironworks/%22 "\\"Ironworks\\""), [textile manufacturing](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Textile_manufacturing/%22 "\\"Textile"), [paper mills](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Paper_mill/%22 "\\"Paper") and [hulling](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Husk/%22 "\\"Husk\\"") mills moving towards steam power.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-batou193_329-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-batou193-329\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>314<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> Economic historian Jean Batou argues that the necessary economic conditions existed in Egypt for the adoption of [oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Oil/%22 "\\"Oil\\"") as a potential energy source for its steam engines later in the 19th century.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-batou193_329-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-batou193-329\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>314<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n1. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-5\%22 "\\"Jump")** *[İslâm Ansiklopedisi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/%C4%B0sl%C3%A2m_Ansiklopedisi/%22 "\\"İslâm")*: \\"It is disputed when the Ottomans conquered this place; Various dates have been put forward in this regard, such as 1361, 1362, 1367 and 1369. Among these, the opinion that Edirne was captured in 1361 as a result of a systematic conquest policy by Murad and Lala Şahin, while Orhan Gazi was still alive, gains prominence. However, it has also been stated that the date of conquest may have occurred after 1366 (1369), based on an elegy showing that the city metropolitan Polykarpos was in Edirne in this capacity until 1366.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-4\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-4\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>4<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n
\\n2. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-9\%22 "\\"Jump")** In Ottoman Turkish, the city was known by various names, among which were *Ḳosṭanṭīnīye* (قسطنطينيه) (replacing the suffix *\-polis* with the Arabic suffix), *Istanbul* (استنبول) and *Islambol* (اسلامبول, lit. 'full of Islam'); see [Names of Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Names_of_Istanbul/%22 "\\"Names")). Kostantiniyye became obsolete in Turkish after the [proclamation of the Republic of Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Proclamation_of_the_Republic_of_Turkey/%22 "\\"Proclamation") in 1923,<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Edhemp286_6-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Edhemp286-6\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>5<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> and after Turkey's transition to Latin script in 1928,<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-7\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>6<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup> the Turkish government in 1930 requested that foreign embassies and companies use *Istanbul*, and that name became widely accepted internationally.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-8\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>7<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n
\\n3. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-10\%22 "\\"Jump")** [Liturgical language](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Liturgical_language/%22 "\\"Liturgical"); among Arabic-speaking citizens\\n
\\n4. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-19\%22 "\\"Jump")** Court, diplomacy, poetry, historiographical works, literary works, taught in state schools, and offered as an elective course or class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>15<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n
\\n5. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-20\%22 "\\"Jump")** Among Greek-speaking community; spoken by some sultans.\\n
\\n6. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-22\%22 "\\"Jump")** Decrees in the 15th century.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-21\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-21\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>16<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n
\\n7. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-24\%22 "\\"Jump")** Foreign language among educated people in the post-[Tanzimat](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Tanzimat/%22 "\\"Tanzimat\\"")/late imperial period.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-0\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Strauss-2010-23\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>17<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n
\\n8. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-26\%22 "\\"Jump")** The sultan from 1512 to 1520.\\n
\\n9. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-27\%22 "\\"Jump")** In 1922, the Ottoman Empire came to an end with the [abolition of the monarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Ottoman_sultanate/%22 "\\"Abolition"), and Abdülmecid, a member of the [Ottoman dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty/%22 "\\"Ottoman"), was elected caliph by the Grand National Assembly of Turkey to replace the last sultan Mehmed VI and held the title under the Ankara Government (1922–1923) and the Republic (1923–1924).\\n
\\n10. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-29\%22 "\\"Jump")** 1 November 1922 marks the formal ending of the Ottoman Empire. Mehmed VI departed Constantinople on 17 November 1922.\\n
\\n11. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-30\%22 "\\"Jump")** The [Treaty of Sèvres](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres/%22 "\\"Treaty") (10 August 1920) afforded a small existence to the Ottoman Empire. On 1 November 1922, the [Grand National Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey/%22 "\\"Grand") (GNAT) abolished the sultanate and declared that all the deeds of the Ottoman regime in Constantinople were null and void as of 16 March 1920, the date of the [occupation of Constantinople](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Occupation_of_Constantinople/%22 "\\"Occupation") under the terms of the Treaty of Sèvres. The international recognition of the GNAT and the [Government of Ankara](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Government_of_the_Grand_National_Assembly/%22 "\\"Government") was achieved through the signing of the [Treaty of Lausanne](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne/%22 "\\"Treaty") on 24 July 1923. The Grand National Assembly of Turkey promulgated the Republic on 29 October 1923.\\n
\\n12. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-35\%22 "\\"Jump")** [Ottoman Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language/%22 "\\"Ottoman"): دولت علیهٔ عثمانیه, [romanized](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Romanization_of_Ottoman_Turkish/%22 "\\"Romanization"): *Devlet-i ʿAlīye-i ʿOsmānīye*, [lit.](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Literal_translation/%22 "\\"Literal") 'Sublime Ottoman State'; [Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Turkish_language/%22 "\\"Turkish"): *Osmanlı İmparatorluğu* or *Osmanlı Devleti*; [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/French_language/%22 "\\"French"): *Empire ottoman*<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-1\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-Strauss-2010-23\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>17<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n
\\n13. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-38\%22 "\\"Jump")** The [Ottoman dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty/%22 "\\"Ottoman") also held the title \\"[caliph](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/List_of_caliphs#Ottoman_Caliphate_\(1517%E2%80%931924\)\%22 "\\"List")\\" from the Ottoman victory over the [Mamluk Sultanate](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate/%22 "\\"Mamluk") in [Ridaniya](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Battle_of_Ridaniya/%22 "\\"Battle") (1517) to the [abolition of the Caliphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Caliphate/%22 "\\"Abolition") (1924) by the Grand National Assembly of Turkey.\\n
\\n14. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-67\%22 "\\"Jump")** A lock-hold on trade between western Europe and Asia is often cited as a primary motivation for [Isabella I of Castile](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile/%22 "\\"Isabella") to fund [Christopher Columbus](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Christopher_Columbus/%22 "\\"Christopher")'s westward journey to find a sailing route to Asia and, more generally, for European seafaring nations to explore alternative trade routes (e.g., K.D. Madan, *Life and travels of Vasco Da Gama* (1998), 9; I. Stavans, *Imagining Columbus: the literary voyage* (2001), 5; W.B. Wheeler and S. Becker, *Discovering the American Past. A Look at the Evidence: to 1877* (2006), 105). This traditional viewpoint has been attacked as unfounded in an influential article by A.H. Lybyer (\\"The Ottoman Turks and the Routes of Oriental Trade\\", *English Historical Review*, 120 (1915), 577–588), who sees the rise of Ottoman power and the beginnings of Portuguese and Spanish explorations as unrelated events. His view has not been universally accepted (cf. K.M. Setton, *The Papacy and the Levant (1204–1571), Vol. 2: The Fifteenth Century (Memoirs of the American Philosophical Society, Vol. 127)* (1978), 335).\\n
\\n15. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-181\%22 "\\"Jump")** Though the revolt was officially initiated on the 10 June, bin Ali's sons ['Ali](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ali_of_Hejaz/%22 "\\"Ali") and [Faisal](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq/%22 "\\"Faisal") had already initiated operations at Medina starting on 5 June.<sup id="\&quot;cite_ref-180\&quot;" class="\&quot;reference\&quot;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_note-180\%22"><span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">[</span>166<span class="\&quot;cite-bracket\&quot;">]</span></a></sup>\\n

\\n8. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-11\%22 "\\"Jump")** Inan, Murat Umut (2019). \\"Imperial Ambitions, Mystical Aspirations: Persian Learning in the Ottoman World\\". In Green, Nile (ed.). *The Persianate World: The Frontiers of a Eurasian Lingua Franca*. University of California Press. pp. 88–89\. As the Ottoman Turks learned Persian, the language and the culture it carried seeped not only into their court and imperial institutions but also into their vernacular language and culture. The appropriation of Persian, both as a second language and as a language to be steeped together with Turkish, was encouraged notably by the sultans, the ruling class, and leading members of the mystical communities.\\n

On the whole, the circumstance in Turkey took a similar course: in Anatolia, the Persian language had played a significant role as the carrier of civilization. \[...\] where it was at time, to some extent, the language of diplomacy \[...\] However Persian maintained its position also during the early Ottoman period in the composition of histories and even Sultan Salim I, a bitter enemy of Iran and the Shi'ites, wrote poetry in Persian. Besides some poetical adaptations, the most important historiographical works are: Idris Bidlisi's flowery \\"Hasht Bihist\\", or Seven Paradises, begun in 1502 by the request of Sultan Bayazid II and covering the first eight Ottoman rulers...\\n

\\n14. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-17\%22 "\\"Jump")** [Yarshater, Ehsan](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ehsan_Yarshater/%22 "\\"Ehsan"); [Melville, Charles](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Charles_P._Melville/%22 "\\"Charles"), eds. \\n15. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-18\%22 "\\"Jump")** Inan, Murat Umut (2019). \\"Imperial Ambitions, Mystical Aspirations: Persian learning in the Ottoman World\\". In [Green, Nile](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Nile_Green/%22 "\\"Nile") (ed.). *The Persianate World The Frontiers of a Eurasian Lingua Franca*. University of California Press. p. 92 (note 27). Though Persian, unlike Arabic, was not included in the typical curriculum of an Ottoman madrasa, the language was offered as an elective course or recommended for study in some madrasas. \\n18. ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-0\%22) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-1\%22) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-2\%22) [Lambton, Ann](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ann_Lambton/%22 "\\"Ann"); [Lewis, Bernard](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Bernard_Lewis/%22 "\\"Bernard") (1995). \\n19. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-28\%22 "\\"Jump")** Pamuk, Şevket (2000). [*A Monetary History of the Ottoman Empire*](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://books.google.com/books?id=Htk3Wn789EQC&pg=PA30\%22). Cambridge University Press. pp. 30–31\. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [0-521-44197-8](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-44197-8/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/0-521-44197-8\\""). [Archived](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151034/https://books.google.com/books?id=Htk3Wn789EQC&pg=PA30\%22) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 21 October 2022. The Ottomans began to strike coins in the name of [Orhan](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Orhan/%22 "\\"Orhan\\"") Bey in 1326. These earliest coins carried inscriptions such as \\"the great Sultan, Orhan son of Osman\\" \[...\] Ottoman historiography has adopted 1299 as the date for the foundation of the state. 1299 might represent the date at which the Ottomans finally obtained their independence from the [Seljuk](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Seljuk_dynasty/%22 "\\"Seljuk") sultan at [Konya](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Konya/%22 "\\"Konya\\""). Probably, they were forced at the same time, or very soon thereafter, to accept the overlordship of the [Ilkhanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Ilkhanids/%22 "\\"Ilkhanids\\"") \[...\] Numismatic evidence thus suggest that independence did not really occur until 1326.\\n

\\n30. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-43\%22 "\\"Jump")** \\n
	- [Kaser 2011](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFKaser2011\%22), p. 336: \\"The emerging Christian nation states justified the prosecution of their Muslims by arguing that they were their former \\"suppressors\\". The historical balance: between about 1820 and 1920, millions of Muslim casualties and refugees back to the remaining Ottoman Empire had to be registered; estimations speak about 5 million casualties and the same number of displaced persons\\"
	\\n- [Fábos 2005](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFF%C3%A1bos2005\%22), p. 437: \\"Muslims had been the majority in Anatolia, the Crimea, the Balkans, and the Caucasus and a plurality in southern Russia and sections of Romania. Most of these lands were within or contiguous with the Ottoman Empire. By 1923, 'only Anatolia, eastern Thrace, and a section of the southeastern Caucasus remained to the Muslim land ... Millions of Muslims, most of them Turks, had died; millions more had fled to what is today Turkey. Between 1821 and 1922, more than five million Muslims were driven from their lands. Five and one-half million Muslims died, some of them killed in wars, others perishing as refugees from starvation and disease' (McCarthy 1995, 1). Since people in the Ottoman Empire were classified by religion, Turks, Albanians, Bosnians, and all other Muslim groups were recognized—and recognized themselves—simply as Muslims. Hence, their persecution and forced migration is of central importance to an analysis of 'Muslim migration.'\\"
	\\n- Schayegh, Cyrus (2024). \\"A Late/Post-Imperial Region of Difference: The Ottoman Empire and its Successor Polities in Southeastern Europe, Turkey, and the Arab East, c. 1850s–1940s\\". *Journal of World History*. **35** (4): 579–622\. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Doi_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"Doi"):[10.1353/jwh.2024.a943172](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://doi.org/10.1353%2Fjwh.2024.a943172/%22). Between 1821 and the 1919–1922 Turko-Greek War, about five and a half million Muslims died of religious-ethnic war-related causes, including disease and hunger during forced migration, in southeastern Europe and the Crimea and Caucasus.
	\\n- [Findley 2008](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFFindley2008\%22), pp. 16, 35
	\\n- [Kirişci 2008](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFKiri%C5%9Fci2008\%22), pp. 175–177
	\\n- [Karpat 2001](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFKarpat2001\%22), p. 343: \\"The main migrations started from Crimea in 1856 and were followed by those from the Caucasus and the Balkans in 1862 to 1878 and 1912 to 1916. These have continued to our day. The quantitative indicators cited in various sources show that during this period a total of about 7 million migrants from Crimea, the Caucasus, the Balkans, and the Mediterranean islands settled in Anatolia. These immigrants were overwhelmingly Muslim, except for a number of Jews who left their homes in the Balkans and Russia in order to live in the Ottoman lands. By the end of the century the immigrants and their descendants constituted some 30 to 40 percent of the total population of Anatolia, and in some western areas their percentage was even higher.\\" ... \\"The immigrants called themselves Muslims rather than Turks, although most of those from Bulgaria, Macedonia, and eastern Serbia descended from the Turkish Anatolian stock who settled in the Balkans in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries.\\"
	\\n- [Karpat 2004](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFKarpat2004\%22), pp. 5–6: \\"Migration was a major force in the social and cultural reconstruction of the Ottoman state in the nineteenth century. While some seven to nine million, mostly Muslim, refugees from lost territories in the Caucasus, Crimea, Balkans and Mediterranean islands migrated to Anatolia and Eastern Thrace, during the last quarter of the nineteenth and the early part of the twentieth centuries...\\"
	\\n- [Pekesen 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFPekesen2012\%22): \\"The immigration had far-reaching social and political consequences for the Ottoman Empire and Turkey.\\" ... \\"Between 1821 and 1922, some 5.3 million Muslims migrated to the Empire.50 It is estimated that in 1923, the year the republic of Turkey was founded, about 25 per cent of the population came from immigrant families.51\\"
	\\n- [Biondich 2011](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFBiondich2011\%22), p. 93: \\"The road from Berlin to Lausanne was littered with millions of casualties. In the period between 1878 and 1912, as many as two million Muslims emigrated voluntarily or involuntarily from the Balkans. When one adds those who were killed or expelled between 1912 and 1923, the number of Muslim casualties from the Balkan far exceeds three million. By 1923 fewer than one million remained in the Balkans\\"
	\\n- [Armour 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFArmour2012\%22), p. 213: \\"To top it all, the Empire was host to a steady stream of Muslim refugees. Russia between 1854 and 1876 expelled 1.4 million Crimean Tartars, and in the mid-1860s another 600,000 Circassians from the Caucasus. Their arrival produced further economic dislocation and expense.\\"
	\\n- [Bosma, Lucassen & Oostindie 2012a](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFBosmaLucassenOostindie2012a\%22), p. 17: \\"In total, many millions of Turks (or, more precisely, Muslim immigrants, including some from the Caucasus) were involved in this 'repatriation' – sometimes more than once in a lifetime – the last stage of which may have been the immigration of seven hundred thousand Turks from Bulgaria between 1940 and 1990. Most of these immigrants settled in urban north-western Anatolia. Today between a third and a quarter of the Republic's population are descendants of these Muslim immigrants, known as Muhacir or Göçmen\\"
	\\n
\\n31. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-44\%22 "\\"Jump")** Ágoston, Gábor (2009). \\"Introduction\\". In Ágoston, Gábor; Bruce Masters (eds.). *Encyclopedia of the Ottoman Empire*.\\n

The scholarly community specializing in Ottoman studies has of late virtually banned the use of \\"Turkey\\", \\"Turks\\", and \\"Turkish\\" from acceptable vocabulary, declaring \\"Ottoman\\" and its expanded use mandatory and permitting its \\"Turkish\\" rival only in linguistic and philological contexts.\\n

\\n66. ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-decline_80-0\%22) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-decline_80-1\%22) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-decline_80-2\%22) Hathaway, Jane (2008). *The Arab Lands under Ottoman Rule, 1516–1800*. Pearson Education Ltd. p. 8. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-0-582-41899-8](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-582-41899-8/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-0-582-41899-8\\""). historians of the Ottoman Empire have rejected the narrative of decline in favor of one of crisis and adaptation; Tezcan, Baki (2010). *The Second Ottoman Empire: Political and Social Transformation in the Early Modern Period*. Cambridge University Press. p. 9. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-1-107-41144-9](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-41144-9/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-1-107-41144-9\\""). Ottomanist historians have produced several works in the last decades, revising the traditional understanding of this period from various angles, some of which were not even considered as topics of historical inquiry in the mid-twentieth century. Thanks to these works, the conventional narrative of Ottoman history – that in the late sixteenth century the Ottoman Empire entered a prolonged period of decline marked by steadily increasing military decay and institutional corruption – has been discarded.; Woodhead, Christine (2011). \\"Introduction\\". In Christine Woodhead (ed.). *The Ottoman World*. Routledge. p. 5. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-0-415-44492-7](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-44492-7/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-0-415-44492-7\\""). Ottomanist historians have largely jettisoned the notion of a post-1600 'decline'\\n

\\n77. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-91\%22 "\\"Jump")** Ágoston, Gábor (2009). \\"Introduction\\". In Ágoston, Gábor; Bruce Masters (eds.). *Encyclopedia of the Ottoman Empire*. p. xxxii.; Faroqhi, Suraiya (1994). \\"Crisis and Change, 1590–1699\\". In İnalcık, Halil; Donald Quataert (eds.). *An Economic and Social History of the Ottoman Empire, 1300–1914*. Vol. 2. Cambridge University Press. p. 553. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-0-521-57456-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-57456-3/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-0-521-57456-3\\""). In the past fifty years, scholars have frequently tended to view this decreasing participation of the sultan in political life as evidence for \\"Ottoman decadence\\", which supposedly began at some time during the second half of the sixteenth century. But recently, more note has been taken of the fact that the Ottoman Empire was still a formidable military and political power throughout the seventeenth century, and that noticeable though limited economic recovery followed the crisis of the years around 1600; after the crisis of the 1683–1699 war, there followed a longer and more decisive economic upswing. Major evidence of decline was not visible before the second half of the eighteenth century.\\n
