\\n228. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-243\%22 "\\"Jump")** Davison, Roderic H. (31 December 1964). [*Reform in the Ottoman Empire, 1856–1876*](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://dx.doi.org/10.1515/9781400878765/%22). [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Doi_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"Doi"):[10.1515/9781400878765](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://doi.org/10.1515%2F9781400878765/%22). [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-1-4008-7876-5](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4008-7876-5/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-1-4008-7876-5\\""). [Archived](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151544/https://www.degruyter.com/document/doi/10.1515/9781400878765/html/%22) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 22 July 2021. There was the ruling Ottoman group, now largely concentrated in the bureaucracy centered on the Sublime Porte, and the mass of the people, mostly peasants. The efendi looked down on \\"the Turk,\\" which was a term of opprobrium indicating boorishness, and preferred to think of himself as an Osmanli. His country was not Turkey, but the Ottoman State. His language was also \\"Ottoman\\"; though he might also call it \\"Turkish,\\" in such a case he distinguished it from *kaba türkçe*, or coarse Turkish, the common speech. His writing included a minimum of Turkish words, except for particles and auxiliary verbs. `{{[cite book](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Template:Cite_book/%22 "\\"Template:Cite")}}`: ISBN / Date incompatibility ([help](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#invalid_isbn_date\%22 "\\"Help:CS1"))\\n

\\n274. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-289\%22 "\\"Jump")** Mignon, Laurent (2005). [*Neither Shiraz nor Paris: papers on modern Turkish literature*](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://books.google.com/books?id=AbQZAQAAIAAJ\%22). Istanbul: ISIS. p. 20. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-975-428-303-7](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-975-428-303-7/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-975-428-303-7\\""). [Archived](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151705/https://books.google.com/books?id=AbQZAQAAIAAJ\%22) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 17 January 2016. Those words could have been readily adopted by Hovsep Vartanyan (1813–1879), the author, who preferred to remain anonymous, of The Story of Akabi (Akabi Hikyayesi), the first novel in Turkish, published with Armenian characters in the same year as [Hisarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Hovhannes_Hisarian/%22 "\\"Hovhannes")'s novel.\\n
\\n275. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-290\%22 "\\"Jump")** Masters, Bruce; Ágoston, Gábor (2009). [*Encyclopedia of the Ottoman Empire*](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://books.google.com/books?id=QjzYdCxumFcC\%22). New York: Facts On File. p. 440. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-1-4381-1025-7](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4381-1025-7/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-1-4381-1025-7\\""). [Archived](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151705/https://books.google.com/books?id=QjzYdCxumFcC\%22) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 20 June 2015. Written in Turkish using the Armenian alphabet, the Akabi History (1851) by Vartan Pasha is considered by some to be the first Ottoman novel.\\n
\\n276. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-291\%22 "\\"Jump")** Pultar, Gönül (2013). [*Imagined identities: identity formation in the age of globalism*](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://books.google.com/books?id=KhiQAwAAQBAJ\%22) (First ed.). \[S.l.\]: Syracuse University Press. p. 329. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-0-8156-3342-6](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8156-3342-6/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-0-8156-3342-6\\""). [Archived](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151705/https://books.google.com/books?id=KhiQAwAAQBAJ\%22) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 17 January 2016. In fact, one of the first Turkish works of fiction in Western-type novel form, Akabi Hikayesi (Akabi's Story), was written in Turkish by Vartan Pasha (born Osep/Hovsep Vartanian/Vartanyan, 1813–1879) and published in Armenian characters in 1851.\\n
\\n277. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-292\%22 "\\"Jump")** Gürçaglar, Şehnaz; Paker, Saliha; Milton, John (2015). *Tradition, Tension, and Translation in Turkey*. John Benjamins Publishing Company. p. 5. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-90-272-6847-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-272-6847-1/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-90-272-6847-1\\""). It is interesting that the first Ottoman novel in Turkish, Akabi Hikayesi (1851, Akabi's Story), was written and published in Armenian letters (for Armenian communities who read in Turkish) by Hovsep Vartanyan (1813–1879), known as Vartan Paşa, a leading Ottoman man of letters and journalist.\\n

\\n285. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-Bloom-2009_300-0\%22 "\\"Jump")** M. Bloom, Jonathan; S. Blair, Sheila, eds. (2009). \\"Ottoman\\". *The Grove Encyclopedia of Islamic Art and Architecture*. Oxford University Press. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\)/%22 "\\"ISBN") [978-0-19-530991-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-530991-1/%22 "\\"Special:BookSources/978-0-19-530991-1\\""). Throughout their history the Ottomans remained supporters of art and artists. Under their patronage a distinctive architectural style developed that combined the Islamic traditions of Anatolia, Iran and Syria with those of the Classical world and Byzantium. The result was a rationalist monumentality that favored spatial unity and architectonic expression.\\n
\\n286. ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-Freely-2011_301-0\%22) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#cite_ref-Freely-2011_301-1\%22) [Freely 2011](https://en.wikipedia.org/%22#CITEREFFreely2011\%22), p. 35 \\"The mosques of the classical period are more elaborate than those of earlier times. They derive from a fusion of a native Turkish tradition with certain elements of the plan of Haghia Sophia, the former cathedral of Constantinople, converted into a mosque in 1453 by Mehmet the Conqueror.\\"\\n
